### [2] SYSTEM PROMPT · 2026-07-08 10:58:49 UTC

```
<ai_inventor_context>
<ai_inventor_summary>
You are one of many LLMs in AI Inventor — an automated research system that generates NOVEL and FEASIBLE hypotheses, investigates them through experiments and research, and produces a paper.

Your output feeds other LLMs downstream. This demands your ABSOLUTE MAXIMUM reasoning — every output must be deeply thought out and maximally useful. Surface-level responses waste downstream computation.
</ai_inventor_summary>

<your_role>
YOU ARE: A research paper writer (Step 3.4: GEN_PAPER_TEXT in the invention loop)

You received the hypothesis, all artifacts, the previous paper draft (if any), and reviewer feedback.
Write a complete paper draft with figure placeholders.

Publication-quality paper → strong contribution. Weak paper → wasted iteration.
</your_role>
</ai_inventor_context>

<research_methodology>
Write like a researcher drafting a paper, not a chatbot summarizing bullet points.

- Structure as a paper would: research question → methodology → results → analysis → limitations. Not a list of "we did X, then Y."
- Ground every claim in specific artifacts and specific numbers. "Results show improvement" is empty — state effect sizes, baselines, and conditions.
- Be honest about what worked, what didn't, and why. Don't spin failures as "future work."
- The paper's headline contribution should be a positive or surprising finding. Negative results are valuable context but should not be the primary narrative — lead with what works.
- Address reviewer feedback from previous iterations explicitly — show you've thought about each critique.
</research_methodology>

<available_tools>
Web research is available through the aii-web-tools skill, in three levels (broad → specific):

1. web search — Returns titles, URLs, snippets. Use first to discover and scan the landscape.
2. web fetch — Reads a page and returns its content as markdown (HTML or PDF). Use to understand a source. May miss specific details — use fetch_grep below if it doesn't find what you need.
3. fetch_grep — Regex search over a page/PDF's full text. Returns exact matching sections with context. Use for precise details, exact numbers, methodology, or PDFs.

Workflow: search → fetch (understand) → fetch_grep (extract specifics).
</available_tools>

<tool_use>
Maximize parallel tool calls. Parallelize independent operations, only sequentialize dependencies.
- Multiple searches/fetches on different topics → parallel in one turn
- Search then fetch results → sequential (need URLs first)
</tool_use>

<system_reminder>
Do not ask follow up questions and do not ask the user anything. Execute all steps independently.
You must follow the todo list provided in each prompt exactly as written.
No placeholders, stubs, or incomplete code — all code must be complete and functional.
</system_reminder>

<process_isolation>
CRITICAL: Multiple pipeline runs may execute simultaneously on this machine. `ps aux | grep method.py` matches ALL runs, not just yours.
- NEVER kill processes by name (`killall`, `pkill -f`, `ps aux | grep ... | xargs kill`). This kills OTHER runs' processes.
- NEVER monitor processes by name (`ps aux | grep method.py`). You will see other runs' processes and get confused.
- ALWAYS use PID-based process management:
  Run: `uv run method.py & PID=$!` or `timeout <seconds> uv run method.py & PID=$!`
  Check: `kill -0 $PID 2>/dev/null && echo "Running" || echo "Ended"`
  Stop: `kill $PID`
  Wait: `wait $PID; echo "Exit code: $?"`
  Monitor: `tail -f logs/run.log & TAIL_PID=$!` then `kill $TAIL_PID` when done
</process_isolation>
```

### [3] SYSTEM-USER prompt · 2026-07-08 10:58:49 UTC

````
<available_domain_handbooks>
Domain handbooks below capture expert knowledge for a specific field — its landscape, prior work, dead ends, evaluation norms, and what counts as a genuinely novel contribution. If one is relevant to your research topic, READ that skill BEFORE proceeding; read the most relevant one(s), or none if none apply. Use it for related-work positioning and how this field frames a genuinely novel contribution.

- **aii-handbook-multi-llm-agents** — Guide for implementing Multi-LLM Agent Systems research using Mirascope orchestration, HuggingFace datasets/evaluation, and proven multi-agent patterns.
</available_domain_handbooks>
<hypothesis>
The research hypothesis.

kind: hypothesis
title: Readability as Circuit Resistance
hypothesis: >-
  Text readability can be accurately modeled through the effective electrical resistance of a discourse graph. When sentences
  are represented as nodes and semantic discourse connections as weighted edges (resistors), the total effective resistance
  (Kirchhoff index) of this graph provides a novel, physically-motivated readability metric. The key insight is that readable
  text creates 'low-resistance pathways' for information flow - coherent discourse with strong semantic connections allows
  information to flow easily (low resistance), while disjointed or complex text impedes information flow (high resistance).
motivation: >-
  Current readability formulas rely on surface features (sentence length, word difficulty) or black-box ML models that don't
  explicitly model the cognitive process of reading. Drawing from electrical network theory, we propose that readable text
  should allow 'easy information flow' through semantic connections, analogous to current flowing through a low-resistance
  circuit. This provides a theoretically grounded, interpretable metric that captures discourse-level coherence in a way surface
  features cannot.
assumptions:
- >-
  Text can be meaningfully represented as a graph where sentences are nodes and edges represent discourse connections (semantic
  similarity, rhetorical relations, or sequential adjacency)
- >-
  The cognitive effort of reading correlates with the 'resistance' to information flow through the discourse graph
- >-
  Edge weights (resistances) can be meaningfully assigned based on semantic distance or discourse relation strength
- >-
  The effective graph resistance (Kirchhoff index) computed from this weighted graph correlates with human judgments of readability
investigation_approach: >-
  1. Construct discourse graphs from texts: nodes=sentences, edges=semantic connections weighted by cosine distance between
  sentence embeddings (or simplified: sequential edges with weights based on position). 2. Compute the effective graph resistance
  (trace of Laplacian pseudoinverse) as the readability score. 3. Evaluate on standard readability datasets (e.g., Weebit,
  CLEAR) by correlating with human ratings. 4. Compare against traditional formulas (Flesch-Kincaid, SMOG) and ML baselines.
  5. Perform ablation: test different graph construction methods (sequential only, similarity-thresholded, full RST parsing).
success_criteria: >-
  The effective resistance metric should: (1) Achieve Pearson correlation r > 0.7 with human readability ratings on standard
  benchmarks, (2) Outperform or match traditional readability formulas (Flesch-Kincaid, etc.) in predictive accuracy, (3)
  Show significant correlation (p < 0.01) with reading time/eye-tracking measures if available, (4) Be computationally feasible
  (< 1s per document for typical lengths). A tiny experiment (N=50 texts with human ratings) showing r > 0.5 would support
  proceeding to full evaluation.
related_works:
- >-
  Mesgar & Strube (2015) 'Graph-based Coherence Modeling For Assessing Readability' - Uses entity grids and discourse relation
  graphs with features like outdegree and frequent subgraphs, but does NOT use effective resistance. Our approach differs
  by using the actual electrical network resistance as the direct readability metric, which captures global information flow
  rather than local graph patterns.
- >-
  Zhang et al. (2026) 'Automatic text readability assessment for educational content based on graph representation learning'
  - Uses GCNs on POS-based dependency graphs. This is a deep learning approach that learns features implicitly, whereas our
  method uses a specific, interpretable graph metric (effective resistance) derived from network theory.
- >-
  Guinaudeau & Strube (2013) 'Graph-based coherence modeling' - Introduces entity graphs and one-mode projections for coherence
  modeling. Our work differs by using effective resistance (a global spectral graph property) rather than local features like
  edge weights or components.
- >-
  Ehret (2018) 'Kolmogorov complexity as a universal measure of language complexity' - Uses compression-based complexity.
  While both approaches use information theory concepts, effective resistance captures discourse-level connectivity while
  Kolmogorov complexity captures lexical/syntactic redundancy.
- >-
  Klein et al. (2025) 'Surprisal Takes It All: Eye Tracking Based Cognitive Evaluation of Text Readability Measures' - Finds
  surprisal (from language models) predicts reading ease. Our approach is complementary: effective resistance models discourse
  structure while surprisal models lexical predictability.
inspiration: >-
  The hypothesis draws from electrical network theory (Kirchhoff's laws, effective resistance) and its application to graph
  analysis. In electrical engineering, the effective resistance between nodes in a network captures how easily current can
  flow. We adapt this to text by treating discourse connections as electrical pathways: coherent, well-connected text has
  low 'resistance' to information flow, while disjointed or complex text has high resistance. This cross-domain transfer from
  circuit theory to readability assessment is, to our knowledge, novel.
terms:
- term: Effective resistance (Kirchhoff index)
  definition: >-
    The sum of resistance distances between all pairs of nodes in a graph, equivalent to the trace of the pseudoinverse of
    the graph Laplacian. In electrical networks, this represents the total resistance 'seen' by current flowing through the
    network.
- term: Graph Laplacian
  definition: >-
    A matrix representation of a graph L = D - A, where D is the degree matrix and A is the adjacency matrix. The pseudoinverse
    of L is used to compute effective resistances between nodes.
- term: Discourse graph
  definition: >-
    A graph representation of text where nodes represent sentences or discourse units, and edges represent rhetorical relations,
    semantic connections, or sequential adjacency.
- term: Resistance distance
  definition: >-
    A graph metric derived from electrical network theory that measures the effective electrical resistance between two nodes
    if unit resistors are placed on each edge. It captures both direct and indirect pathways between nodes.
- term: Kirchhoff index
  definition: >-
    The sum of all pairwise resistance distances in a graph. It is a global graph invariant that measures the overall 'connectivity'
    or 'flow capacity' of the network.
summary: >-
  We propose a novel readability metric based on effective electrical resistance of discourse graphs. By modeling text as
  a circuit where sentences are connected by semantic pathways, the total resistance to information flow (Kirchhoff index)
  provides a physically-motivated, interpretable measure of readability that captures discourse-level coherence beyond surface
  features.
</hypothesis>

<all_artifacts>
FULL EVIDENCE BASE: All 3 research artifacts across all iterations.

--- Item 1 ---
id: art_IM3J27ltI7Pm
type: research
title: Effective Resistance for Readability Graphs Research
summary: >-
  This research artifact provides comprehensive findings on three foundational components for implementing a 'Readability
  as Circuit Resistance' metric: (1) Computing effective resistance (Kirchhoff index) from graph Laplacian pseudoinverse -
  identified NetworkX built-in functions (resistance_distance, effective_graph_resistance) and netneurotools library with
  implementation code examples and numerical stability considerations. (2) Constructing discourse graphs from text - documented
  three edge construction methods (sequential, similarity-based with SBERT, RST-based), edge weighting schemes, and relevant
  Python libraries (sentence-transformers, NetworkX, RST parsers). (3) Identifying readability benchmark datasets - compiled
  detailed comparison of 5 datasets (WeeBit, CLEAR, Newsela, OneStopEnglish, Wikilarge) with sizes, rating scales, access
  methods, and licensing information. The artifact includes an implementation roadmap with specific library recommendations,
  sample code for computing resistance scores from text, and an evaluation plan using the CLEAR corpus. Confidence levels
  are assessed for each finding, and follow-up research questions are provided.

  the 2 best datasets for the experiment. The selected datasets are: 1) SetFit/onestop_english (567 examples, 3-class classification:
  Elementary/Intermediate/Advance) - an established benchmark with published paper (Vajala and Lučić, 2018), and 2) agentlans/readability

--- Item 3 ---
id: art_rfw45zxdqTLX
type: experiment
title: Circuit Resistance Readability Test
summary: >-
  Implemented and evaluated a novel effective resistance metric for text readability using discourse graphs. The method constructs
  a graph where sentences are nodes and edges represent discourse connections (sequential in this implementation). The Kirchhoff
  index (sum of effective resistances between all node pairs) serves as the readability score. Evaluated on 50 synthetic texts
  with known readability levels (simple/medium/complex). Results show strong correlation with human readability scores (Pearson
  r=0.80, Spearman ρ=0.81, p<0.001) and competitive performance with baseline metrics (Flesch-Kincaid r=0.87, SMOG r=0.85).
  The method is computationally efficient (0.004s per document) and provides a theoretically grounded alternative to traditional
  readability formulas. Limitations: current implementation uses sequential graphs only (no semantic similarity edges) due
  to environment constraints; future work should incorporate sentence embeddings for enhanced performance.
workspace_path: >-
  /ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_RiHQW43yNEvO/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_experiment_1
out_expected_files:
- method.py
- full_method_out.json
- mini_method_out.json
- preview_method_out.json
</all_artifacts>

confusion matrices / scatter plots.

### [4] HUMAN-USER prompt · 2026-07-08 10:58:49 UTC

```
Propose a simple, novel, testable ML method for scoring text readability and validate it with a tiny experiment.
```

### [5] THINKING · 2026-07-08 10:58:59 UTC

```
Let me break down this task carefully. I'm being asked to write a research paper draft based on:

1. A hypothesis about "Readability as Circuit Resistance" - modeling text readability through electrical resistance of discourse graphs
2. Three artifacts that contain research, dataset, and experiment results
3. This is the FIRST paper draft

Let me understand what I need to do:
1. Follow the aii-paper-writing and aii-semscholar-bib skills
2. Do literature review using web search tools
3. Read all artifacts carefully to extract concrete details
4. Write the full paper with figure placeholders
5. Output as JSON

Let me start by setting up my todo list and then proceeding systematically.

The hypothesis is about modeling text readability using effective electrical resistance (Kirchhoff index) of discourse graphs. The key insight is that readable text creates "low-resistance pathways" for information flow.

From the artifacts:
1. Research artifact (art_IM3J27ltI7Pm) - foundational components for implementing the metric
2. Dataset artifact (art_vYMmBxe-2BfW) - collected readability datasets
3. Experiment artifact (art_rfw45zxdqTLX) - implemented and evaluated the metric

The experiment results show:
- Pearson r=0.80, Spearman ρ=0.81, p<0.001 correlation with human readability scores
- Flesch-Kincaid r=0.87, SMOG r=0.85 as baselines
- Computational efficiency: 0.004s per document

{"content": [{"cache_prompt": false, "type": "text", "text": "## Technical Papers\n\nGuidance for the standard \"technical paper\" format: propose a method/system/framework, evaluate it experimentally, report results. This is the main track at most CS venues (NeurIPS, ICML, ICLR, ACL, AAAI, etc.). Does NOT cover: pure theory/formal proofs, survey papers, position papers, or dataset/benchmark papers \u2014 those have different structures.\n\n### Paper Structure\n\nTarget 6-8 pages. Use formal academic language, third person. Support claims with evidence from artifacts.\n\n#### Rough Page Budget (8-page paper)\n\n| Section | Pages | Notes |\n|---|---|---|\n| Abstract | 0.3 | Problem, approach, key result |\n| Introduction | 1.0-1.5 | The most important section |\n| Related Work | 0.5-1.0 | Beginning or end (see below) |\n| Methods | 1.5-2.0 | Architecture fig on page 1 |\n| Experiments | 1.5-2.0 | Setup + results + ablations |\n| Discussion | 0.5-1.0 | Limitations go here |\n| Conclusion | 0.3-0.5 | Do not repeat the abstract |\n| References | 0.5-1.0 | Not counted in page limit |\n\n**Critical rule**: A clear new technical contribution must be articulated by page 3 (quarter of the paper). If the reader doesn't know what you did by then, you've lost them.\n\n#### Section Details\n\n**Abstract** (150-250 words): State the problem, your approach, and the main results. Be factual and comprehensive. Do not repeat the abstract word-for-word later in the paper.\n\n**Introduction** \u2014 Follow this 5-paragraph structure:\n\n1. **What is the problem?** Define the task concretely.\n2. **Why is it interesting and important?** Real-world impact, scale.\n3. **Why is it hard?** Why do naive approaches fail?\n4. **Why hasn't it been solved before?** What's wrong with prior solutions? How does yours differ?\n5. **What are the key components of your approach and results?** Include specific limitations.\n\nEnd with a \"Summary of Contributions\" subsection \u2014 bullet list of contributions with section references. This doubles as an outline, saving space.\n\n**Related Work** \u2014 Placement decision:\n- **Beginning** (Section 2): If it can be short yet detailed, or if you need a strong defensive stance against prior work early.\n- **End** (before Conclusions): If comparisons require your technical content, or if it can be summarized briefly in the Introduction. Can be titled \"Discussion and Related Work.\"\n\n**Methods/Approach**: Every section tells a story \u2014 the story of the results, NOT the story of how you arrived at them. Use top-down description: readers should see where the material is going and be able to skip ahead. Move gory details to appendices.\n\n**Experiments**: Setup (datasets, metrics, baselines) \u2192 main results \u2192 ablations \u2192 analysis. Every claim needs quantitative evidence.\n\n**Discussion**: Interpret results, compare to prior work, state limitations honestly. Limitations should be specific and actionable, not vague disclaimers.\n\n**Conclusion**: Short summarizing paragraph. Do NOT repeat material from the Abstract or Introduction. Make original claims more concrete (e.g., reference quantitative results). Include future work as bullet list \u2014 if actively pursuing follow-up, say so to mark territory.\n\n#### Writing Quality Rules\n\n- Define all notation/terminology before use, only once. Group global definitions in Preliminaries.\n- Do NOT use nonreferential \"this\", \"that\", \"these\", \"it\". Always specify the referent. BAD: \"This is important because...\" GOOD: \"This accuracy gap is important because...\"\n- Do NOT use \"etc.\" unless remaining items are completely obvious. BAD: \"We measure volatility, scalability, etc.\" GOOD: \"We measure volatility and scalability.\"\n- Do NOT write \"for various reasons\" \u2014 state the actual reasons.\n- \"That\" is defining, \"which\" is nondefining. \"The algorithms that are easy to implement\" vs \"The algorithms, which are easy to implement.\"\n- Use italics for definitions and quotes, not for emphasis. Context alone should provide emphasis.\n\n### Figure Format\n\nFigures use a hybrid marker + structured array approach. ALL figures are generated by a separate pipeline step using an AI image model \u2014 your `image_gen_detailed_description` is the ONLY input that model sees. It cannot read files or access data. Do NOT generate actual image files yourself (no matplotlib, no PIL, no image generation scripts).\n\n**In paper_text**: Place `[FIGURE:fig_id]` markers where figures should appear.\n\n**In figures array**: Provide full specs as structured objects with these fields:\n- `id` \u2014 matches the `[FIGURE:id]` marker in paper_text\n- `title` \u2014 short descriptive title\n- `caption` \u2014 LaTeX caption that appears below the figure in the paper\n- `image_gen_detailed_description` \u2014 detailed prompt for the image generator (axes, ALL values, colors, layout)\n- `summary` \u2014 brief summary of what the figure communicates\n\nExample in paper_text:\n```\n...our method achieves state-of-the-art results as shown below.\n\n[FIGURE:fig_1]\n\nThe results in Figure 1 demonstrate...\n```\n\nExample figure spec in figures array:\n```json\n{\"id\": \"fig_1\", \"title\": \"Performance Comparison\", \"caption\": \"Comparison of geometric mean query latency across optimizers on JOB benchmark. RLQOpt achieves 2.3x speedup over PostgreSQL.\", \"image_gen_detailed_description\": \"Grouped bar chart. X-axis: model names. Y-axis: accuracy (0.0-1.0). Values: ModelA=0.847, ModelB=0.762, Baseline=0.531. Error bars with std: 0.02, 0.03, 0.05. Sans-serif font, white background.\", \"summary\": \"Compares accuracy of proposed methods vs baseline.\"}\n```\n\nEvery marker in text MUST have a matching figure in the array, and vice versa.\n\n#### Data Precision Requirement\n\n`image_gen_detailed_description` MUST include exact numbers from artifact output files. Read the actual output files before writing figure specs.\n\n- BAD: \"Compare accuracy metrics across configurations\"\n- GOOD: \"Grouped bar chart. X-axis: model names. Y-axis: accuracy (0.0-1.0). Values: K=3: 0.765, K=5: 0.729, Baseline: 0.121.\"\n\n#### Figure vs Table Decision\n\nDo NOT create figures for tabular data (rows/columns of text or numbers). Use `\\begin{table}` in LaTeX instead. Figures are for actual visualizations only (charts, plots, diagrams).\n\n#### Figure Placement Strategy\n\nBe intentional with figure ordering. The architectural/method overview figure explaining the proposed approach MUST appear early \u2014 in the Introduction or at the start of Methods \u2014 so readers can immediately orient themselves. Readers skim papers top-down; if the first figure they see is a results bar chart, they have no mental model for interpreting it.\n\nRecommended ordering:\n1. **Architecture/method diagram** \u2014 Introduction or early Methods (so readers understand the approach before diving into details)\n2. **Conceptual/analogy figures** \u2014 Introduction or Methods (to build intuition)\n3. **Results figures** (bar charts, line plots, scatter plots) \u2014 Results section\n4. **Analysis/ablation figures** \u2014 Discussion or later Results\n\n#### Guidelines\n\n- Plan 3-6 figures total across the paper\n- Place [FIGURE:fig_id] markers INLINE where referenced in text\n- Include axes, labels, ALL numeric values in figure descriptions\n- Both data-driven figures (bar charts, line plots) and conceptual diagrams (architecture, flowcharts)\n- Be as detailed as possible in descriptions: specify aspect ratio, preferred colors, all data values, axis labels, ranges, legend entries, and any other visual details. The more specific the description, the better the generated figure\n\n### Bibliography with Semantic Scholar\n\nBuild `./references.bib` using the aii-semscholar-bib skill (real BibTeX from Semantic Scholar):\n\n1. Collect DOIs, ArXiv IDs, or titles for all papers you need to cite\n2. Call `aii_semscholar_bib__fetch` with the full list in one batch\n3. Write the returned .bib text into `./references.bib`\n\nRules:\n- Do NOT fabricate BibTeX entries \u2014 always fetch from Semantic Scholar\n- If a paper isn't found (very recent preprint), write the entry manually as fallback\n- Use `\\bibliography{references}` and `\\bibliographystyle{plainnat}`\n- Do NOT use inline `thebibliography` environment\n\n### Citation Format (for Research Artifacts)\n\nWhen writing research with numbered citations:\n\n1. Every factual claim MUST have a numbered citation: `[1]`, `[2]`, `[1, 3]`, etc.\n2. Each source in the \"sources\" array MUST have an \"index\" field\n3. The index MUST EXACTLY MATCH citation numbers in the text\n4. NEVER cite a number without a matching source index\n5. Example: \"LLMs show 40% improvement with multi-agent collaboration [1].\"\n\n---\nThis skill is located at `/ai-inventor/.claude/skills/aii-paper-writing`. Any files it references (e.g. under `scripts/`, `references/`, `assets/`) are relative to that directory."}], "is_error": false, "skill_name": "aii-paper-writing", "kind": "InvokeSkillObservation"}

{"content": [{"cache_prompt": false, "type": "text", "text": "## Tool: `aii_semscholar_bib__fetch`\n\nBatch-fetch BibTeX entries from Semantic Scholar. Pass all references in a single call \u2014 the tool handles batching internally.\n\n### How it works\n\n1. **DOI/ArXiv refs** \u2192 batched into POST /paper/batch calls (up to 500 per API call, auto-chunked)\n2. **Title-only refs** \u2192 individual GET /paper/search/match (1s delay between)\n3. **Post-process** \u2192 fix entry type, fix citation key (AuthorYYYY), inject DOI\n\nThe ability server runs a single worker (`max_threads: 1`). Multiple concurrent tool calls are queued \u2014 each runs independently (no cross-request aggregation). Batching happens within each request.\n\n### Input format\n\n```json\n{\n  \"references\": [\n    {\"doi\": \"10.48550/arXiv.1706.03762\", \"author\": \"Vaswani\", \"year\": 2017},\n    {\"arxiv\": \"2201.11903\", \"author\": \"Wei\", \"year\": 2022},\n    {\"title\": \"Tree of Thoughts\", \"author\": \"Yao\", \"year\": 2023}\n  ]\n}\n```\n\nEach reference object can have:\n- `doi` \u2014 DOI string (ArXiv DOIs like `10.48550/arXiv.XXXX.XXXXX` auto-convert to ArXiv IDs)\n- `arxiv` \u2014 ArXiv ID (e.g. `\"2305.14325\"`)\n- `title` \u2014 Paper title (used for search/match when no DOI/ArXiv)\n- `author` \u2014 First author last name (for cleaner citation key)\n- `year` \u2014 Publication year (int, for citation key)\n\nAt least one of `doi`, `arxiv`, or `title` is required per reference.\n\n### Output format\n\n```json\n{\n  \"success\": true,\n  \"bib_text\": \"@inproceedings{Vaswani2017, ...}\\n\\n@article{Wei2022, ...}\",\n  \"total\": 3,\n  \"found\": 3,\n  \"failed_count\": 0,\n  \"entries\": [{\"citation_key\": \"Vaswani2017\", \"bibtex\": \"...\", \"title\": \"...\", \"doi\": \"...\", \"arxiv\": \"\"}],\n  \"failed\": []\n}\n```\n\n### Workflow\n\n1. Collect DOIs, ArXiv IDs, or titles for all papers you need to cite\n2. Call `aii_semscholar_bib__fetch` with the full list in **one call**\n3. Save `bib_text` from the response to your `references.bib` file\n4. Check `failed` \u2014 for any missed papers, follow the **fallback procedure** below\n\n### Fallback for failed references (MANDATORY)\n\nNEVER fabricate BibTeX. For each failed reference:\n1. **WebSearch** for `\"Title\" author year` (try `site:arxiv.org` too)\n2. **WebFetch** the paper page \u2192 extract title, authors, year, venue, DOI/ArXiv ID\n3. If DOI/ArXiv found \u2192 retry `aii_semscholar_bib__fetch` with it\n4. Last resort: write BibTeX by hand using **only verified info from the actual paper page**\n\n---\n\n### CLI (for manual use / debugging)\n\n```bash\nSKILL_DIR=\"$(git rev-parse --show-toplevel 2>/dev/null || echo /ai-inventor)/.claude/skills/aii-semscholar-bib\" && \\\n$SKILL_DIR/../.ability_client_venv/bin/python $SKILL_DIR/scripts/aii_semscholar_bib__fetch.py --refs '[\n  {\"doi\": \"10.48550/arXiv.1706.03762\", \"author\": \"Vaswani\", \"year\": 2017},\n  {\"arxiv\": \"2201.11903\", \"author\": \"Wei\", \"year\": 2022},\n  {\"title\": \"Tree of Thoughts\", \"author\": \"Yao\", \"year\": 2023}\n]'\n```\n\n`--json, -j` \u2014 output raw JSON instead of .bib text\n\n**If the script fails** with a connection error (ability server not running): create a local `.venv`, install server deps from `server_requirements.txt` into it, then import the `@aii_ability` function from the script and call it directly \u2014 bypassing the server:\n```bash\nuv venv .venv --python=3.12 && uv pip install --python=.venv/bin/python -r \"$SKILL_DIR/scripts/server_requirements.txt\"\n```\n\n---\nThis skill is located at `/ai-inventor/.claude/skills/aii-semscholar-bib`. Any files it references (e.g. under `scripts/`, `references/`, `assets/`) are relative to that directory."}], "is_error": false, "skill_name": "aii-semscholar-bib", "kind": "InvokeSkillObservation"}

Identified NetworkX built-in functions (resistance_distance, effective_graph_resistance) and netneurotools library. Documented three discourse graph construction methods: sequential edges, similarity-based edges using SBERT embeddings, and RST-based edges using rhetorical structure parsers. Compiled detailed comparison of 5 benchmark readability datasets (WeeBit, CLEAR, Newsela, OneStopEnglish, Wikilarge) with sizes, access methods, and licensing. Provided implementation roadmap with specific library recommendations and code examples.\",\n     5\t  \"out_expected_files\": {\n     6\t    \"output\": \"research_out.json\"\n     7\t  },\n     8\t  \"upload_ignore_regexes\": [],\n     9\t  \"answer\": \"## Executive Summary\\n\\nThis research investigated three foundational components for implementing a 'Readability as Circuit Resistance' metric: (1) computing effective resistance from graph Laplacian, (2) constructing discourse graphs from text, and (3) identifying benchmark datasets for evaluation.\\n\\n## 1. Effective Resistance Computation\\n\\n### 1.1 Mathematical Foundation\\nThe Kirchhoff index (effective graph resistance) is defined as the sum of resistance distances between all pairs of nodes in a graph [1, 2]. Mathematically, it equals the trace of the Moore-Penrose pseudoinverse of the Laplacian matrix L\u207a:\\n\\nKf(G) = \u03a3\u1d62\u2c7c R\u1d62\u2c7c = n\u00b7tr(L\u207a)\\n\\nwhere R\u1d62\u2c7c is the resistance distance between nodes i and j, and n is the number of nodes [1, 2].\\n\\n### 1.2 Python Implementations\\n\\n**NetworkX (Recommended for simplicity)**\\n- `nx.effective_graph_resistance(G)` - directly computes Kirchhoff index [1]\\n- `nx.resistance_distance(G, nodeA, nodeB)` - computes resistance between two nodes [1]\\n- Both functions handle weight inversion automatically (invert_weight=True by default) [1]\\n- Implementation based on Theorem 2.2 in Ellens et al. (2011) [1]\\n\\n**netneurotools library**\\n- `netneurotools.metrics.effective_resistance(W)` - computes full effective resistance matrix [3]\\n- Requires weight matrix W as input\\n- Uses numpy.linalg.pinv for pseudoinverse computation [3]\\n\\n**Manual Implementation**\\n```python\\nimport networkx as nx\\nimport numpy as np\\nfrom scipy import sparse\\nfrom scipy.sparse.linalg import pinv\\n\\n# Construct Laplacian\\nL = nx.laplacian_matrix(G).toarray()\\n\\n# Compute pseudoinverse (handle singularity)\\nL_pinv = pinv(L)\\n\\n# Kirchhoff index = trace of pseudoinverse\\nkirschhoff_index = np.trace(L_pinv) * G.number_of_nodes()\\n```\\n\\n### 1.3 Numerical Stability Considerations\\n- Laplacian matrix is singular (smallest eigenvalue = 0) [4]\\n- Use scipy.sparse.linalg.pinv or numpy.linalg.pinv for stable pseudoinverse [4]\\n- For large graphs, consider approximation algorithms (arXiv:2310.05527) [5]\\n- netneurotools warns 'Test before use' for effective_resistance function [3]\\n\\n## 2. Discourse Graph Construction\\n\\n### 2.1 Graph Construction Methods\\n\\n**Method 1: Sequential Edges (Baseline)**\\n- Connect sentences in sequential order (i to i+1)\\n- Simple, captures local coherence\\n- Weight: uniform (1.0) or position-based decay\\n\\n**Method 2: Similarity-Based Edges (Recommended)**\\n- Compute cosine similarity between sentence embeddings\\n- Add edge if similarity > threshold (e.g., 0.3)\\n- Weight: 1 - cosine_distance or similarity value\\n\\n*Implementation with sentence-transformers:*\\n```python\\nfrom sentence_transformers import SentenceTransformer\\nimport numpy as np\\n\\nmodel = SentenceTransformer('all-MiniLM-L6-v2')\\nsentences = text.split('.')\\nembeddings = model.encode(sentences)\\n\\n# Compute cosine similarity matrix\\nsimilarity_matrix = np.inner(embeddings, embeddings)\\n\\n# Add edges where similarity > threshold\\nfor i in range(len(sentences)):\\n    for j in range(i+1, len(sentences)):\\n        if similarity_matrix[i][j] > 0.3:\\n            G.add_edge(i, j, weight=similarity_matrix[i][j])\\n```\\n\\n**Method 3: RST-Based Edges (Advanced)**\\n- Use Rhetorical Structure Theory to identify discourse relations\\n- Available parsers: isanlp_rst, feng-hirst-rst-parser [6]\\n- Captures hierarchical document structure\\n- Edge weight based on relation strength\\n\\n### 2.2 Edge Weighting Schemes\\n\\n1. **Cosine distance**: 1 - cosine_similarity between SBERT embeddings\\n2. **Position-based**: decay with sentence distance (e.g., exp(-|i-j|/\u03c3))\\n3. **Discourse relations**: strength from RST parser confidence\\n4. **Hybrid**: combine multiple signals\\n\\n### 2.3 Libraries and Tools\\n\\n- **sentence-transformers**: SBERT embeddings [7]\\n- **NetworkX**: graph construction and analysis [1]\\n- **spaCy**: sentence tokenization, dependency parsing\\n- **isanlp_rst**: RST discourse parser [6]\\n- **feng-hirst-rst-parser**: Python RST parser (PyPI package) [6]\\n\\n## 3. Readability Benchmark Datasets\\n\\n### 3.1 Dataset Comparison Table\\n\\n| Dataset | Size | Rating Scale | Access Method | License |\\n|----------|------|--------------|----------------|---------|\\n| **WeeBit** | 6,388 texts | 5 grade levels (ages 7-16) | GitHub (shlomihod/deep-text-eval) | Research use |\\n| **CLEAR** | ~5,000 excerpts | Continuous scores (3rd-12th) | GitHub (scrosseye/CLEAR-Corpus) | CC BY-NC-SA 4.0 |\\n| **Newsela** | 1,911 articles | 5 levels (2nd-12th grade) | Request at newsela.com/legal/data | Proprietary |\\n| **OneStopEnglish** | 189 texts \u00d7 3 levels | 3 levels (Elem, Inter, Adv) | GitHub (nishkalavallabhi/OneStopEnglishCorpus) | Research use |\\n| **Wikilarge** | ~296,402 sentences | Simplification pairs | HuggingFace (waboucay/wikilarge) | Various |\\n\\n### 3.2 Detailed Dataset Profiles\\n\\n**WeeBit Corpus**\\n- Sources: WeeklyReader magazine, BBC Bitesize [8]\\n- 5 balanced classes with 625 documents each [8]\\n- Age range: 7-16 years\\n- Preprocessing code available\\n- GitHub: shlomihod/deep-text-eval\\n\\n**CLEAR Corpus (Recommended)**\\n- 5,000 text excerpts from 250+ years of literature [9]\\n- Two genres: informational and literary\\n- Unique readability scores (not just levels)\\n- Meta-data: publishing year, genre\\n- Direct GitHub download (Excel format)\\n- License: CC BY-NC-SA 4.0 DEED\\n\\n**Newsela Corpus**\\n- Each article rewritten at 4-5 reading levels [8]\\n- Same topic at different complexities (reduces topic correlation)\\n- Access: requires formal request to Newsela\\n- URL: newsela.com/legal/data\\n- Used in multiple papers [8]\\n\\n**OneStopEnglish Corpus**\\n- 189 topics \u00d7 3 levels = 567 texts total [10]\\n- Levels: Elementary, Intermediate, Advanced\\n- Public GitHub repository with CSV format\\n- Good for text simplification research\\n\\n## 4. Implementation Roadmap\\n\\n### 4.1 Recommended Approach\\n\\n1. **Graph Construction**: Similarity-based edges with SBERT embeddings\\n   - Balance between simplicity and capturing semantic structure\\n   - Threshold: 0.3-0.5 (tune on validation set)\\n\\n2. **Resistance Computation**: Use NetworkX built-in functions\\n   - `nx.effective_graph_resistance()` for Kirchhoff index\\n   - Handles edge cases (disconnected graphs) automatically\\n\\n3. **Evaluation Dataset**: CLEAR corpus\\n   - Open access (GitHub)\\n   - Continuous scores (better for regression)\\n   - Good size (5,000 examples)\\n   - Clear licensing (CC BY-NC-SA)\\n\\n### 4.2 Code Structure\\n\\n```python\\nimport networkx as nx\\nfrom sentence_transformers import SentenceTransformer\\n\\nclass ReadabilityResistanceScorer:\\n    def __init__(self, similarity_threshold=0.3):\\n        self.model = SentenceTransformer('all-MiniLM-L6-v2')\\n        self.threshold = similarity_threshold\\n    \\n    def text_to_graph(self, text):\\n        # Tokenize into sentences\\n        sentences = text.split('.')\\n        \\n        # Create graph\\n        G = nx.Graph()\\n        \\n        # Add nodes\\n        for i, sent in enumerate(sentences):\\n            G.add_node(i, sentence=sent)\\n        \\n        # Add similarity-based edges\\n        embeddings = self.model.encode(sentences)\\n        for i in range(len(sentences)):\\n            for j in range(i+1, len(sentences)):\\n                sim = np.dot(embeddings[i], embeddings[j])\\n                if sim > self.threshold:\\n                    G.add_edge(i, j, weight=sim)\\n        \\n        return G\\n    \\n    def compute_resistance_score(self, text):\\n        G = self.text_to_graph(text)\\n        # Kirchhoff index (effective graph resistance)\\n        R_eff = nx.effective_graph_resistance(G)\\n        return R_eff\\n```\\n\\n### 4.3 Evaluation Plan\\n\\n1. **Dataset**: CLEAR corpus (train/test split)\\n2. **Metrics**: Pearson correlation, RMSE, MAE vs. human ratings\\n3. **Baselines**: Flesch-Kincaid, SMOG, BERT-based regressor\\n4. **Ablations**: \\n   - Sequential vs. similarity-based edges\\n   - Different similarity thresholds\\n   - Weight schemes (uniform, cosine, hybrid)\\n\\n## 5. Key Findings and Recommendations\\n\\n### 5.1 Do's\\n- Use NetworkX's built-in `effective_graph_resistance()` function [1]\\n- Apply SBERT (sentence-transformers) for semantic similarity [7]\\n- Start with CLEAR corpus for evaluation (open access, good size) [9]\\n- Normalize resistance scores (e.g., divide by n_nodes\u00b2) for comparability\\n\\n### 5.2 Don'ts\\n- Don't use directed graphs (NetworkX functions require undirected) [1]\\n- Don't assume all edges should be weighted equally\\n- Don't ignore disconnected components (effective resistance is infinite)\\n- Don't use Newsela as primary dataset (access restrictions)\\n\\n### 5.3 Open Questions\\n- Optimal similarity threshold for edge construction?\\n- How to handle very short texts (fewer than 3 sentences)?\\n- Does resistance correlate with traditional readability formulas?\\n- Can we interpret resistance values intuitively (e.g., lower = more readable)?\\n\\n## 6. Confidence Assessment\\n\\n**High Confidence** (strong evidence):\\n- NetworkX has stable, well-documented functions for resistance computation [1]\\n- CLEAR corpus is publicly accessible via GitHub [9]\\n- sentence-transformers provides reliable SBERT embeddings [7]\\n\\n**Medium Confidence** (needs verification):\\n- RST parsers usability (limited documentation found) [6]\\n- Optimal graph construction method for readability (multiple valid approaches)\\n- Numerical stability for large graphs (>1000 sentences)\\n\\n**Low Confidence** (speculative):\\n- Whether resistance metric will correlate with human judgments\\n- Computational efficiency for real-time applications\\n- Generalizability across text genres\\n\\n## References\\n\\n[1] NetworkX Documentation - resistance_distance and effective_graph_resistance. https://networkx.org/documentation/stable/reference/algorithms/generated/networkx.algorithms.distance_measures.effective_graph_resistance.html\\n\\n[2] Ellens, W., et al. (2) Constructing discourse graphs from text - documented three edge construction methods (sequential, similarity-based with SBERT, RST-based), edge weighting schemes, and relevant Python libraries (sentence-transformers, NetworkX, RST parsers). (3) Identifying readability benchmark datasets - compiled detailed comparison of 5 datasets (WeeBit, CLEAR, Newsela, OneStopEnglish, Wikilarge) with sizes, rating scales, access methods, and licensing information. The artifact includes an implementation roadmap with specific library recommendations, sample code for computing resistance scores from text, and an evaluation plan using the CLEAR corpus. Confidence levels are assessed for each finding, and follow-up research questions are provided.\n     6\t\n     7\t## Research Findings\n     8\t\n     9\t## Comprehensive Research Findings\n    10\t\n    11\t### 1. Effective Resistance Computation\n    12\t\n    13\tThe Kirchhoff index (effective graph resistance) is computed as the sum of resistance distances between all node pairs in a graph, which equals n\u00b7tr(L\u207a) where L\u207a is the Moore-Penrose pseudoinverse of the Laplacian matrix [1, 2]. \n    14\t\n    15\t**Python Implementation Options:**\n    16\t\n    17\t1. **NetworkX (Recommended)**: Provides built-in `nx.effective_graph_resistance(G)` and `nx.resistance_distance(G, nodeA, nodeB)` functions [1]. These handle weight inversion automatically (invert_weight=True), use the algorithm from Ellens et al. (2011) [1], and raise appropriate errors for disconnected graphs.\n    18\t\n    19\t2. **netneurotools**: Offers `effective_resistance(W)` function that computes full resistance matrix using numpy.linalg.pinv [3]. Documentation warns to 'Test before use' [3].\n    20\t\n    21\t3. **Manual Implementation**: Construct Laplacian with `nx.laplacian_matrix(G)`, compute pseudoinverse with `scipy.sparse.linalg.pinv(L)`, then Kirchhoff index = trace(L\u207a) \u00d7 n [4]. Laplacian is singular so regular inverse cannot be used [4].\n    22\t\n    23\t**Numerical Stability**: For large graphs (>1000 nodes), consider fast approximation algorithms that estimate diagonal entries of pseudoinverse in nearly linear time [5].\n    24\t\n    25\t### 2. Discourse Graph Construction Methods\n    26\t\n    27\t**Method 1: Sequential Edges** - Connect sentences in order (i to i+1). Simple baseline capturing local coherence but misses long-distance semantic relationships.\n    28\t\n    29\t**Method 2: Similarity-Based Edges (Recommended)** - Compute cosine similarity between sentence embeddings and add edges where similarity > threshold (e.g., 0.3) [7]. Implementation using sentence-transformers library:\n    30\t```python\n    31\tfrom sentence_transformers import SentenceTransformer\n    32\tmodel = SentenceTransformer('all-MiniLM-L6-v2')\n    33\tembeddings = model.encode(sentences)\n    34\t# Add edges where cosine similarity > threshold\n    35\t```\n    36\tThis method balances simplicity with ability to capture semantic structure [7].\n    37\t\n    38\t**Method 3: RST-Based Edges** - Use Rhetorical Structure Theory parsers (e.g., isanlp_rst [6], feng-hirst-rst-parser) to identify discourse relations and construct edges based on rhetorical structure. Most linguistically motivated but complex to implement [6].\n    39\t\n    40\t**Edge Weighting Schemes**: (1) Cosine distance: weight = 1 - cosine_distance, (2) Position-based: exponential decay with sentence distance, (3) Discourse relation strength from RST parser, (4) Hybrid combinations.\n    41\t\n    42\t### 3. Readability Benchmark Datasets\n    43\t\n    44\t**Dataset Comparison:**\n    45\t\n    46\t| Dataset | Size | Rating Scale | Access | License |\n    47\t|----------|------|--------------|--------|---------|\n    48\t| **CLEAR** | ~5,000 excerpts | Continuous (3rd-12th grade) | GitHub (scrosseye/CLEAR-Corpus) [9] | CC BY-NC-SA 4.0 |\n    49\t| **WeeBit** | 6,388 texts (3,125 downsampled) | 5 levels (ages 7-16) | GitHub (shlomihod/deep-text-eval) [8] | Research use |\n    50\t| **Newsela** | 1,911 articles | 5 levels (2nd-12th grade) | Request at newsela.com [8] | Proprietary |\n    51\t| **OneStopEnglish** | 189 texts \u00d7 3 levels | 3 levels (Elem/Inter/Adv) | GitHub (nishkalavallabhi/OneStopEnglishCorpus) [10] | Research use |\n    52\t| **Wikilarge** | ~296,402 sentences | Simplification pairs | HuggingFace (waboucay/wikilarge) | Various |\n    53\t\n    54\t**Recommendation**: Use **CLEAR corpus** for primary evaluation because it has open access via GitHub [9], good size (5,000 examples), continuous readability scores (better for regression), and clear licensing (CC BY-NC-SA 4.0) [9].\n    55\t\n    56\t### 4. Implementation Roadmap\n    57\t\n    58\t**Graph Construction**: Use similarity-based edges with SBERT embeddings [7], threshold 0.3-0.5 (tune on validation set).\n    59\t\n    60\t**Resistance Computation**: Use NetworkX's `effective_graph_resistance()` function [1] - simple API, handles edge cases, well-documented.\n    61\t\n    62\t**Evaluation**: CLEAR corpus [9], metrics: Pearson correlation, RMSE, MAE vs. human ratings, baselines: Flesch-Kincaid, SMOG, BERT-based regressor.\n    63\t\n    64\t**Sample Code Structure**:\n    65\t```python\n    66\timport networkx as nx\n    67\tfrom sentence_transformers import SentenceTransformer\n    68\t\n    69\tclass ReadabilityResistanceScorer:\n    70\t    def __init__(self, threshold=0.3):\n    71\t        self.model = SentenceTransformer('all-MiniLM-L6-v2')\n    72\t        self.threshold = threshold\n    73\t    \n    74\t    def text_to_graph(self, text):\n    75\t        sentences = [s.strip() for s in text.split('.') if s.strip()]\n    76\t        G = nx.Graph()\n    77\t        for i, sent in enumerate(sentences):\n    78\t            G.add_node(i, sentence=sent)\n    79\t        embeddings = self.model.encode(sentences)\n    80\t        for i in range(len(sentences)):\n    81\t            for j in range(i+1, len(sentences)):\n    82\t                sim = np.dot(embeddings[i], embeddings[j])\n    83\t                if sim > self.threshold:\n    84\t                    G.add_edge(i, j, weight=sim)\n    85\t        return G\n    86\t    \n    87\t    def compute_resistance_score(self, text):\n    88\t        G = self.text_to_graph(text)\n    89\t        return nx.effective_graph_resistance(G)\n    90\t```\n    91\t\n    92\t### 5. Key Recommendations\n    93\t\n    94\t**Do's:**\n    95\t- Use NetworkX's built-in functions for resistance computation [1]\n    96\t- Apply SBERT (sentence-transformers) for semantic similarity [7]\n    97\t- Start with CLEAR corpus for evaluation [9]\n    98\t- Normalize resistance scores for comparability (e.g., divide by n\u00b2)\n    99\t\n   100\t**Don'ts:**\n   101\t- Don't use directed graphs (NetworkX functions require undirected) [1]\n   102\t- Don't assume all edges should have equal weight\n   103\t- Don't ignore disconnected components (effective resistance is infinite)\n   104\t- Don't rely solely on Newsela (access requires approval) [8]\n   105\t\n   106\t### 6. Confidence Assessment\n   107\t\n   108\t**High Confidence** (strong evidence from multiple sources):\n   109\t- NetworkX has stable, well-documented resistance functions [1]\n   110\t- CLEAR corpus is publicly accessible via GitHub [9]\n   111\t- SBERT provides reliable sentence embeddings [7]\n   112\t\n   113\t**Medium Confidence** (needs empirical validation):\n   114\t- Optimal graph construction method (multiple valid approaches)\n   115\t- Similarity threshold selection (0.3-0.5 range suggested)\n   116\t- Resistance score interpretation (hypothesis: lower = more readable)\n   117\t\n   118\t**Low Confidence** (speculative, requires experimentation):\n   119\t- Whether resistance metric will correlate with human judgments\n   120\t- Computational efficiency for real-time applications\n   121\t- Generalizability across different text genres and domains\n   122\t\n   123\t## Sources\n   124\t\n   125\t[1] [NetworkX effective_graph_resistance Documentation](https://networkx.org/documentation/stable/reference/algorithms/generated/networkx.algorithms.distance_measures.effective_graph_resistance.html) \u2014 Official documentation for NetworkX function to compute Kirchhoff index (effective graph resistance) with implementation details, parameters, and examples.\n   126\t\n   127\t[2] [Effective graph resistance - Ellens et al. 2011](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0024379511001443) \u2014 Foundational paper defining effective graph resistance, its computation from Laplacian pseudoinverse, and applications in graph analysis.\n   128\t\n   129\t[3] [netneurotools effective_resistance Documentation](https://netneurotools.readthedocs.io/en/latest/generated/netneurotools.metrics.effective_resistance.html) \u2014 Documentation for alternative Python implementation of effective resistance computation using numpy.linalg.pinv for Laplacian pseudoinverse.\n   130\t\n   131\t[4] [NumPy pinv Documentation](https://numpy.org/doc/stable/reference/generated/numpy.linalg.pinv.html) \u2014 Documentation for Moore-Penrose pseudoinverse computation using SVD, relevant for handling singular Laplacian matrices.\n   132\t\n   133\t[5] [Diagonal of Pseudoinverse of Graph Laplacian - Lu et al. 2023](https://arxiv.org/abs/2310.05527) \u2014 Recent paper on fast approximation algorithms for computing diagonal entries of Laplacian pseudoinverse, relevant for large graphs.\n   134\t\n   135\t[6] [isanlp_rst RST Discourse Parser](https://github.com/tchewik/isanlp_rst) \u2014 GitHub repository for Rhetorical Structure Theory discourse parser that can be used for linguistically-motivated graph construction.\n   136\t\n   137\t[7] [Sentence Transformers Documentation](https://sbert.net/) \u2014 Library for computing sentence embeddings (SBERT) used for semantic similarity-based graph construction with pre-trained transformer models.\n   138\t\n   139\t[8] [Linguistic Features for Readability Assessment - Deutsch et al. 2020](https://arxiv.org/pdf/2006.00377) \u2014 Comprehensive paper describing WeeBit and Newsela corpora with details on dataset construction, accessibility, and readability assessment approaches.\n   140\t\n   141\t[9] [CLEAR Corpus GitHub Repository](https://github.com/scrosseye/CLEAR-Corpus) \u2014 Official repository for CommonLit Ease of Readability (CLEAR) corpus with ~5,000 excerpts, readability scores, and metadata available under CC BY-NC-SA 4.0 license.\n   142\t\n   143\t[10] [OneStopEnglish Corpus - Vajjala & Lu\u010di\u0107 2018](https://aclanthology.org/W18-0535/) \u2014 Paper introducing OneStopEnglish corpus with 189 texts at three reading levels (Elementary, Intermediate, Advanced) for readability assessment and text simplification.\n   144\t\n   145\t## Follow-up Questions\n   146\t\n   147\t- What is the optimal similarity threshold for constructing similarity-based edges in discourse graphs for readability assessment, and how does it vary across different text genres?\n   148\t- How does the Kirchhoff index correlate with traditional readability metrics (Flesch-Kincaid, SMOG) across different types of text (narrative, expository, technical)?\n   149\t- Can the resistance metric be computed efficiently for long documents (>50 sentences) using approximation algorithms, and what accuracy is lost compared to exact computation?\n   150\t\n   151\t---\n   152\t*Generated by AI Inventor Pipeline*\n   153\t\n"}], "is_error": false, "command": "view", "path": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_RiHQW43yNEvO/3_invention_loop/iter_1/gen_art/gen_art_research_1/research_report.md", "prev_exist": true, "old_content": null, "new_content": null, "kind": "FileEditorObservation"}

156\t    # Pseudoinverse using scipy.linalg.pinv\n   157\t    try:\n   158\t        L_pinv = scipy.linalg.pinv(L)\n   159\t    except Exception as e:\n   160\t        logger.warning(f\"Pseudoinverse computation failed: {e}\")\n   161\t        return 0.0, {**metadata, \"error\": str(e)}\n   162\t\n   163\t    # Kirchhoff index: n * trace(L_pinv)\n   164\t    try:\n   165\t        kirchhoff_index = 170\t            for j in range(i + 1, n):\n   171\t                r_ij = L_pinv[i, i] + L_pinv[j, j] - 2 * L_pinv[i, j]\n   172\t                kirchhoff_index += r_ij\n   173\t\n   174\t ### [45] THINKING · 2026-07-08 10:59:53 UTC

```
The key results I need to extract:

From the experiment artifact (gen_art_experiment_1):
- Effective resistance correlation: Pearson r=0.7975, p<4.14e-12, Spearman ρ=0.8122, p<8.11e-13
- MAE=0.523, RMSE=0.636
- Flesch-Kincaid: r=0.8706, p<2.13e-16, ρ=0.8174
- SMOG: r=0.8490, p<6.70e-15, ρ=0.8586
- Coleman-Liau: r=0.8630, p<7.67e-16, ρ=0.8143
- Avg sentence length: r=0.8216, p<2.64e-13, ρ=0.7855
- Avg word length: r=0.8596, p<1.34e-15, ρ=0.8184
- Computational performance: avg 0.00325s per doc
- Ablation: sequential only (same as main result since only sequential was implemented)
- Dataset: 50 synthetic texts with known readability levels (simple/medium/complex)

Wait, looking at the data more carefully, I notice that the effective resistance values are all -1.333 or 0.0. This suggests the sequential graph implementation has issues - for 3-sentence texts the resistance is -1.333 (which is actually n*tr(L+)/n = tr(L+) for a path graph of 3 nodes), and for 1-sentence texts it's 0.0.

Actually, re-reading the method.py code, I see the issue - the Kirchhoff index for a path graph with n nodes is (n-1)*(n+1)/3 for unweighted edges... but more importantly, the code is using a path graph (sequential edges only), and the normalization might be problematic.
 
The perfect correlation of 0.7975 between effective resistance and human scores seems suspicious given the limited variation in resistance values. With only two distinct resistance values (-1.333 for multi-sentence texts and 0.0 for single-sentence texts), achieving such a high correlation requires careful examination of how the scores align with the human readability ratings.

The negative sign convention is counterintuitive—the code negates the Kirchhoff index so higher values indicate more difficult texts, but this creates an interpretation problem since lower resistance should theoretically mean more readable text (easier information flow). The sign handling needs clarification.

I'm noticing the experimental design局限性: it relies on synthetic text rather than established benchmarks like WeeBit or CLEAR, and the single-sentence complex texts all receive 0.0 resistance, constraining the metric's discrimination ability. The current graphite shows the method is promising but requires validation on authentic readability datasets with multi-sentence examples to properly evaluate the resistance-readability relationship.

Now I need to search for the referenced papers to build the bibliography and related work section. I've identified several key citations: Ellens et al. 2011 on effective graph resistance, the Mesgar & Strube 2015 graph-based coherence paper, Zhang et al. 2026 on graph representation learning for readability, Guinaudeau & Strube 2013 on graph-based coherence modeling, Ehret 2018 on Kolmogorov complexity, and Klein et al. 2025 on surprisal and eye-tracking. Let me search for these to get the proper BibTeX entries and verify the details

However, traditional models mainly rely on basic language features and often miss deeper patterns in organizing and expressing information. While rapid advancements in different Artificial Intelligence subfields, especially modern text embedding methods, have significantly improved readability assessment tools, existing models still need improvement. This is particularly true for educational texts, where continuous scoring is more effective than fixed-level classification to address diverse learner needs. This study proposes a graph-based method for readability assessment using Graph Convolutional Networks (GCNs) and a novel graph construction technique to represent textual structures. It models textual complexity by incorporating syntactic dependencies and assigning edge weights based on the part-of-speech tags of intermediate words. Additionally, Bayesian Optimization is used to fine-tune hyperparameters and graph construction configurations, improving the robustness and accuracy of the final model. Tested on the CLEAR dataset, the proposed method achieved an \\\\(R^2\\\\) score of 0.9729. Also, the proposed method was evaluated on a classification-based dataset, and the predicted scores in the same classes were meaningfully close. The results confirm the effectiveness of the proposed method in modeling text complexity and producing accurate readability scores for educational purposes.\n\n### Similar content being viewed by others\n\n###  [The usage of artificial Intelligence-empowered text analysis model with convolutional neural network in english reading ](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41598-025-26720-8?fromPaywallRec=false)\n\nArticle Open access 28 November 2025\n\n###  [Application of artificial intelligence graph convolutional network in classroom grade evaluation ](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41598-025-17903-4?fromPaywallRec=false)\n\nArticle Open access 01 September 2025\n\n###  [Enhancing educational assessment through automated question classification using a RoBERTa-based ensemble model ](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41598-026-45486-1?fromPaywallRec=false)\n\nArticle Open access 23 March 2026\n\n## Introduction\n\nAutomatic readability assessment is the quantitative evaluation of how difficult a text is to read and comprehend for a specific target audience[1](/articles/s41598-026-41313-9#ref-CR1 \"Vajjala, S. Thus far, the development and evaluation of such methods have primarily relied on two types of offline behavioral data, performance on reading comprehension tests and ratings of text readability levels. In this work, we instead focus on a fundamental and understudied aspect of readability, real-time reading ease, captured with online reading measures using eye tracking. We introduce an evaluation framework for readability scoring methods which quantifies their ability to account for reading ease, while controlling for content variation across texts. Applying this evaluation to prominent traditional readability formulas, modern machine learning systems, frontier Large Language Models and commercial systems used in education, suggests that they are all poor predictors of reading ease in English. This outcome holds across native and non-native speakers, reading regimes, and textual units of different lengths. The evaluation further reveals that existing methods are often outperformed by word properties commonly used in psycholinguistics for prediction of reading times. Our results highlight a fundamental limitation of existing approaches to readability scoring, the utility of psycholinguistics for readability research, and the need for new, cognitively driven readability scoring approaches that can better account for reading ease.\n\nSubjects: |  Computation and Language (cs.CL)\n---|---\nCite as: | [arXiv:2502.11150](https://arxiv.org/abs/2502.11150) [cs.CL]\n  | (or  [arXiv:2502.11150v4](https://arxiv.org/abs/2502.11150v4) [cs.CL] for this v"}], "is_error": false, "command": "cd /ai-inventor && export SKILL_DIR=\"$(git rev-parse --show-toplevel 2>/dev/null || echo /ai-inventor)/.claude/skills/aii-web-tools\" && export PY=\"$SKILL_DIR/../.ability_client_venv/bin/python\" && $PY \"$SKILL_DIR/scripts/aii_fast_web_fetch.py\" fetch --url \"https://arxiv.org/abs/2502.11150\" --max-chars 3000", "exit_code": 0, "timeout": false, "metadata": {"exit_code": 0, "pid": -1, "username": "root", "hostname": "e79179696dde", "working_dir": "/ai-inventor", "py_interpreter_path": "/usr/local/bin/python", "prefix": "", "suffix": "\n[The command completed with exit code 0.]"}, "full_output_save_dir": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_RiHQW43yNEvO/.oh_sessions/c4f484470a3042f7a33efff8629ecc34/observations", "kind": "TerminalObservation"}

Pass all references in a single call \u2014 the tool handles batching internally.\n\n### How it works\n\n1. **DOI/ArXiv refs** \u2192 batched into POST /paper/batch calls (up to 500 per API call, auto-chunked)\n2. **Title-only refs** \u2192 individual GET /paper/search/match (1s delay between)\n3. **Post-process** \u2192 fix entry type, fix citation key (AuthorYYYY), inject DOI\n\nThe ability server runs a single worker (`max_threads: 1`). Multiple concurrent tool calls are queued \u2014 each runs independently (no cross-request aggregation). Batching happens within each request.\n\n### Input format\n\n```json\n{\n  \"references\": [\n    {\"doi\": \"10.48550/arXiv.1706.03762\", \"author\": \"Vaswani\", \"year\": 2017},\n    {\"arxiv\": \"2201.11903\", \"author\": \"Wei\", \"year\": 2022},\n    {\"title\": \"Tree of Thoughts\", \"author\": \"Yao\", \"year\": 2023}\n  ]\n}\n```\n\nEach reference object can have:\n- `doi` \u2014 DOI string (ArXiv DOIs like `10.48550/arXiv.XXXX.XXXXX` auto-convert to ArXiv IDs)\n- `arxiv` \u2014 ArXiv ID (e.g. `\"2305.14325\"`)\n- `title` \u2014 Paper title (used for search/match when no DOI/ArXiv)\n- `author` \u2014 First author last name (for cleaner citation key)\n- `year` \u2014 Publication year (int, for citation key)\n\nAt least one of `doi`, `arxiv`, or `title` is required per reference.\n\n### Output format\n\n```json\n{\n  \"success\": true,\n  \"bib_text\": \"@inproceedings{Vaswani2017, ...}\\n\\n@article{Wei2022, ...}\",\n  \"total\": 3,\n  \"found\": 3,\n  \"failed_count\": 0,\n  \"entries\": [{\"citation_key\": \"Vaswani2017\", \"bibtex\": \"...\", \"title\": \"...\", \"doi\": \"...\", \"arxiv\": \"\"}],\n  \"failed\": []\n}\n```\n\n### Workflow\n\n1. Collect DOIs, ArXiv IDs, or titles for all papers you need to cite\n2. Call `aii_semscholar_bib__fetch` with the full list in **one call**\n3. Save `bib_text` from the response to your `references.bib` file\n4. Check `failed` \u2014 for any missed papers, follow the **fallback procedure** below\n\n### Fallback for failed references (MANDATORY)\n\nNEVER fabricate BibTeX. For each failed reference:\n1. **WebSearch** for `\"Title\" author year` (try `site:arxiv.org` too)\n2. **WebFetch** the paper page \u2192 extract title, authors, year, venue, DOI/ArXiv ID\n3. If DOI/ArXiv found \u2192 retry `aii_semscholar_bib__fetch` with it\n4. {"content": [{"cache_prompt": false, "type": "text", "text": "URL: https://math.leidenuniv.nl/scripties/EllensMaster.pdf\nType: PDF\nLength: 116779 chars (truncated)\n\n--- Content ---\n\nE\ufb00ective resistance\nand other graph measures for network robustness\nWendy Ellens\nApril 29, 2011\nMaster thesis\nMathematical Institute, University of Leiden\nTNO ICT, Delft\nThesis supervisors\nDr. F.M. Spieksma\nDr. ir. A. Jamakovi\u00b4c\nProf. dr. ir. R.E. Kooij\n\nii\n\nAbstract\nRobustness is the ability of a network to continue performing well when it is subject to failures\nor attacks. In this thesis we survey robustness measures on simple, undirected and unweighted\ngraphs, network failures being interpreted as vertex or edge deletions. We study graph mea-\nsures based on connectivity, distance, betweenness and clustering.\nBesides these, reliability\npolynomials and measures based on the Laplacian eigenvalues are considered.\nIn addition to surveying existing measures, we propose a new robustness measure, the nor-\nmalized e\ufb00ective resistance, which is derived from the total e\ufb00ective resistance. Total e\ufb00ective\nresistance is \u2014 within the \ufb01eld of electric circuit analysis \u2014 de\ufb01ned as the sum of the pairwise\ne\ufb00ective resistances over all pairs of vertices. The strength of this measure lies in the fact that\nall (not necessarily disjoint) paths are considered, in other words, the more backup possibilities,\nthe larger the normalized e\ufb00ective resistance and the larger the robustness. A chapter is dedi-\ncated to optimizing the normalized e\ufb00ective resistance, \ufb01rst for graphs with a \ufb01xed number of\nvertices and diameter, and second for the addition of an edge to a given graph.\nFor all of the measures described above we evaluate the e\ufb00ectiveness as a measure of network\nrobustness. The discussion and comparison of robustness measures is illustrated by a number\nof examples. Where possible we make extensions to weighted graphs and for all statements we\nprovide either an elaboration of the original proof, or \u2014 when a rigorous proof is not available \u2014\nwe provide one ourselves.\nKeywords: network robustness; graph measures; Laplacian eigenvalues; graph spectrum; e\ufb00ec-\ntive resistance; optimization\nAMS classi\ufb01cation: 05C50; 05C81; 05C90; 94C05; 94C15\niii\n\niv\nABSTRACT\n\nPreface\nTo conclude the Master program in Mathematics at the University of Leiden, I have carried\nout a nine-month research project at TNO Information and Communication Technology. This\nthesis is a mathematical report of this project and as such supposes some knowledge about\ngraph theory, linear algebra and Markov chains.\nI would like to thank my TNO supervisors Almerima Jamakovi\u00b4c and Robert Kooij for their\nguidance and con\ufb01dence, and my university supervisor Floske Spieksma for the time we spent\ntogether solving major and minor mathematical problems.\nI would also like to say thanks\nto Piet Van Mieghem, chairman of the Network Architecture and Service section of the Delft\nUniversity of Technology, for his interest in my project, his spontaneous emails containing\nresearch questions, and his collaboration in the paper \u2018E\ufb00ective graph resistance\u2019 that Piet,\nFloske, Almerima, Rob and I wrote together.\nIt has been a plea"}], "is_error": false, "command": "cd /ai-inventor && export SKILL_DIR=\"$(git rev-parse --show-toplevel 2>/dev/null || echo /ai-inventor)/.claude/skills/aii-web-tools\" && export PY=\"$SKILL_DIR/../.ability_client_venv/bin/python\" && $PY \"$SKILL_DIR/scripts/aii_fast_web_fetch.py\" fetch --url \"https://math.leidenuniv.nl/scripties/EllensMaster.pdf\" --max-chars 3000", "exit_code": 0, "timeout": false, "metadata": {"exit_code": 0, "pid": -1, "username": "root", "hostname": "e79179696dde", "working_dir": "/ai-inventor", "py_interpreter_path": "/usr/local/bin/python", "prefix": "", "suffix": "\n[The command completed with exit code 0.]"}, "full_output_save_dir": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_RiHQW43yNEvO/.oh_sessions/c4f484470a3042f7a33efff8629ecc34/observations", "kind": "TerminalObservation"}

{"content": [{"cache_prompt": false, "type": "text", "text": "URL: http://www.christianbentz.de/MLC2018/Ehret.pdf\nType: PDF\nLength: 14890 chars (truncated)\n\n--- Content ---\n\nKOLMOGOROV COMPLEXITY AS A UNIVERSAL MEASURE OF\nLANGUAGE COMPLEXITY\nKatharina Ehret\nkehret@sfu.ca\nDepartment of Linguistics, Simon Fraser University, Burnaby, Canada\nThis paper presents an unsupervised information-theoretic measure that is a promising candi-\ndate for becoming a universally applicable metric of language complexity. The measure boils\ndown to Kolmogorov complexity and uses compression programs to assess the complexity in\ntext samples via their information content. Generally, better compression rates indicate lower\ncomplexity. In this paper, the measure is applied to a typological dataset of 37 languages cover-\ning 7 different language families. Speci\ufb01cally, overall, morphological and syntactic complexity\nare measured. The results often coincide with intuitive complexity judgements, e.g. Afrikaans\nis overall comparatively simple, Turkish is morphologically complex. Yet, in some cases the\nresults are surprising, e.g. Chinese turns out to be morphologically highly complex. It is con-\ncluded that the method needs further adaptation for the application to different writing systems.\nDespite this caveat, the method is in principle applicable to all types of languages.\n1. Introduction\nLanguage complexity is a very fashionable research topic in the typological-\nsociolinguistics community (Baechler & Seiler, 2016; Baerman, Brown, & Cor-\nbett, 2015; Kortmann & Szmrecsanyi, 2012; Sampson, 2009; Miestamo, 2008).\nTheoretical complexity research is concerned with the de\ufb01nition and measure-\nment of language complexity, and the reasons for variation in language complex-\nity. Most of this research analyses complexity variation in cross-linguistic datasets\n(e.g. Nichols, 1992) or different varieties of the same language (e.g. Szmrecsanyi,\n2009; Trudgill, 2009). Despite the plethora of research on language complexity,\nno universally applicable de\ufb01nition or metric of complexity exists. Thus, it is vir-\ntually impossible to compare complexity measurements across different studies.\nAgainst this backdrop, this paper presents an unsupervised information-\ntheoretic measure of language complexity, which has the potential of becoming\na universally applicable metric of complexity. This measure, also dubbed the\ncompression technique (see Ehret, 2017), was \ufb01rst introduced by Juola (1998)\nand substantially extended by Ehret (2017), Ehret and Szmrecsanyi (2016), and\nEhret (2014). The measure is based on the notion of Kolmogorov complexity and\nmeasures the information content of a string by the length of the shortest possi-\n8\n\nble description that is required to (re)construct the exact string (Li, Chen, Li, Ma,\n& Vit\u00b4anyi, 2004; Juola, 2008). The two strings below, for example, both count\nten symbols. String (1-a) can be compressed to four symbols. In contrast, the\nshortest description of string (1-b) is the string itself, which counts ten symbols.\nString (1-a) is therefore less complex than string (1-b).\n(1)\na.\npkpkpkpkpk (10 symbols) \u00a9 5\u00d7gh (4 symbols)\nb.\nc4pk?9ag"}], "is_error": false, "command": "cd /ai-inventor && export SKILL_DIR=\"$(git rev-parse --show-toplevel 2>/dev/null || echo /ai-inventor)/.claude/skills/aii-web-tools\" && export PY=\"$SKILL_DIR/../.ability_client_venv/bin/python\" && $PY \"$SKILL_DIR/scripts/aii_fast_web_fetch.py\" fetch --url \"http://www.christianbentz.de/MLC2018/Ehret.pdf\" --max-chars 3000", "exit_code": 0, "timeout": false, "metadata": {"exit_code": 0, "pid": -1, "username": "root", "hostname": "e79179696dde", "working_dir": "/ai-inventor", "py_interpreter_path": "/usr/local/bin/python", "prefix": "", "suffix": "\n[The command completed with exit code 0.]"}, "full_output_save_dir": "/ai-inventor/aii_data/runs/run_RiHQW43yNEvO/.oh_sessions/c4f484470a3042f7a33efff8629ecc34/observations", "kind": "TerminalObservation"}
